\textbf{Row Limit Selectors:}

Both history tables include a ``Rows'' dropdown to control how many
records are displayed (25, 50, 100, 250, or 500).

\textbf{Auto-refresh:}

Enable the checkbox to automatically refresh the dashboard every 60
seconds - useful for monitoring incremental generation and Dynamic Table
refreshes in real-time.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Real-Time Dynamic Table History}, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, rightrule=.15mm, arc=.35mm, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, left=2mm, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, breakable, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, opacitybacktitle=0.6, colback=white, coltitle=black, leftrule=.75mm]

The Dynamic Table refresh history uses Snowflake's
\texttt{INFORMATION\_SCHEMA.DYNAMIC\_TABLE\_REFRESH\_HISTORY()} table
function, which provides real-time data with no latency. This shows both
completed and in-progress refreshes.

\end{tcolorbox}

\hypertarget{common-workflows}{%
\section{Common Workflows}\label{common-workflows}}

\hypertarget{first-time-setup}{%
\subsection{First-Time Setup}\label{first-time-setup}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Connect} - Select your Snowflake connection and click Connect
\item
  \textbf{Initial Load} - Go to 📥 Initial Load, set scale to 0.01,
  click Generate \& Load
\item
  \textbf{Verify} - Go to 📊 Overview to confirm data was loaded
\item
  \textbf{Public Datasets} (optional) - Load any additional datasets
  needed
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{testing-dynamic-tables}{%
\subsection{Testing Dynamic Tables}\label{testing-dynamic-tables}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Complete first-time setup above
\item
  \textbf{Deploy Generator} - Go to 🔄 Incremental Generator, click
  Deploy Generator
\item
  \textbf{Start Task} - Click Start Task to begin continuous generation
\item
  \textbf{Monitor} - Go to 📈 Monitoring to watch data grow
\item
  \textbf{Stop Task} - When done testing, click Stop Task
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{creating-a-dev-environment}{%
\subsection{Creating a Dev
Environment}\label{creating-a-dev-environment}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Complete first-time setup with production-scale data (scale 0.1 or
  1.0)
\item
  \textbf{Create Branch} - Go to 🌿 Development Branch
\item
  Set sample percentage (e.g., 10\%)
\item
  Click \textbf{Create Development Branch}
\item
  Use \texttt{FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV} for development/testing
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{troubleshooting}{%
\section{Troubleshooting}\label{troubleshooting}}

\hypertarget{app-wont-start}{%
\subsection{App won't start}\label{app-wont-start}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Check Streamlit is installed}
\ExtensionTok{pip}\NormalTok{ install streamlit}

\CommentTok{\# Try a different port}
\ExtensionTok{streamlit}\NormalTok{ run app.py }\AttributeTok{{-}{-}server.port}\NormalTok{ 8502}

\CommentTok{\# Check for import errors}
\ExtensionTok{python} \AttributeTok{{-}c} \StringTok{"from core import DataManager"}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{connection-fails}{%
\subsection{Connection fails}\label{connection-fails}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Verify your connection works with SnowSQL or another tool
\item
  Check \texttt{\textasciitilde{}/.snowflake/connections.toml} format
\item
  Ensure your IP is allowlisted if using network policies
\item
  Try manual credentials instead of named connection
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{no-tables-found}{%
\subsection{``No tables found''}\label{no-tables-found}}

The database/schema doesn't exist or has no tables. Use \textbf{Initial
Load} to create and populate tables.

\hypertarget{incremental-generator-not-running}{%
\subsection{Incremental generator not
running}\label{incremental-generator-not-running}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Check task status - it may be SUSPENDED
\item
  Verify the task was deployed (click Deploy Generator)
\item
  Check EXECUTE TASK privilege on your role
\item
  Review task history for errors
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{cli-equivalents}{%
\section{CLI Equivalents}\label{cli-equivalents}}

Each page shows a \textbf{CLI Equivalent} section with the corresponding
command-line commands. This is useful for:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Automation and scripting
\item
  CI/CD pipelines
\item
  Reproducibility
\item
  Users who prefer command-line interfaces
\end{itemize}

The Streamlit UI and CLI tools use the same underlying \texttt{core.py}
module, ensuring consistent behavior.

\part{Appendix B: Environment Setup}

\hypertarget{environment-setup-1}{%
\chapter{Environment Setup}\label{environment-setup-1}}

Security model, databases, and role configuration

\hfill\break

\hypertarget{overview-17}{%
\section{Overview}\label{overview-17}}

This appendix provides setup scripts to create the Snowflake environment
used throughout the Feature Store Implementation Guide. The setup
follows a \textbf{layered approach} that mirrors real enterprise
environments:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Admin setup} - Creates databases, roles, warehouse (requires
  ACCOUNTADMIN)
\item
  \textbf{DEV setup} - Creates tables, deploys generator (requires
  \texttt{FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE})
\item
  \textbf{Consumer Quickstart} - Demonstrates consumer capabilities
  (requires Consumer role)
\end{enumerate}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Single User, Multiple Roles}, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, rightrule=.15mm, arc=.35mm, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, left=2mm, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, breakable, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, opacitybacktitle=0.6, colback=white, coltitle=black, leftrule=.75mm]

For learning purposes, we use a single service user
(\texttt{FS\_DEMO\_USER}) with three roles that can be switched as
needed. In production, you would typically have separate users for each
role.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Switch between roles as needed}
\KeywordTok{USE} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE;      }\CommentTok{{-}{-} Administrative tasks}
\KeywordTok{USE} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FS\_DEV\_ROLE;      }\CommentTok{{-}{-} Feature development  }
\KeywordTok{USE} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FS\_CONSUMER\_ROLE; }\CommentTok{{-}{-} Query and training}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{tcolorbox}

\hypertarget{quick-start-4}{%
\section{Quick Start}\label{quick-start-4}}

\hypertarget{prerequisites-3}{%
\subsection{Prerequisites}\label{prerequisites-3}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Snowflake account with ACCOUNTADMIN access (for bootstrap only)
\item
  SnowSQL CLI or Snowsight worksheet access
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{step-1-bootstrap-existing-accountadmin}{%
\subsection{Step 1: Bootstrap (Existing
ACCOUNTADMIN)}\label{step-1-bootstrap-existing-accountadmin}}

Run the minimal bootstrap script with your existing admin user to create
\texttt{FS\_DEMO\_USER}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{snowsql} \AttributeTok{{-}f}\NormalTok{ scripts/00\_bootstrap.sql}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This creates the \texttt{FS\_DEMO\_USER} and grants SYSADMIN,
SECURITYADMIN, and ACCOUNTADMIN roles.

\hypertarget{step-2-admin-setup-fs_demo_user}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Step 2: Admin setup
(\texttt{FS\_DEMO\_USER})}{Step 2: Admin setup (FS\_DEMO\_USER)}}\label{step-2-admin-setup-fs_demo_user}}

Log in as \texttt{FS\_DEMO\_USER} and run the main setup:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{snowsql} \AttributeTok{{-}a} \OperatorTok{\textless{}}\NormalTok{account}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ {-}u FS\_DEMO\_USER }\AttributeTok{{-}r}\NormalTok{ ACCOUNTADMIN }\AttributeTok{{-}f}\NormalTok{ scripts/01\_dba\_setup.sql}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This creates:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  User: \texttt{FS\_DEMO\_USER}
\item
  Roles: \texttt{FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE}, \texttt{FS\_DEV\_ROLE},
  \texttt{FS\_CONSUMER\_ROLE}
\item
  Warehouse: \texttt{FS\_DEV\_WH} (X-Small) --- default for this guide
  (matches main chapters; larger deployments often add dedicated
  warehouses for training or DT refresh)
\item
  API Integration: \texttt{FS\_GIT\_API\_INTEGRATION} - GitHub
  integration for Git-backed development
\item
  Databases: \texttt{FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO},
  \texttt{FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{step-3-dev-setup-fs_admin_role}{%
\subsection{Step 3: DEV Setup
(FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE)}\label{step-3-dev-setup-fs_admin_role}}

Switch to \texttt{FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE} and run the dev setup to create
tables and deploy the generator:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{snowsql} \AttributeTok{{-}a} \OperatorTok{\textless{}}\NormalTok{account}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ {-}u FS\_DEMO\_USER }\AttributeTok{{-}r}\NormalTok{ FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE }\AttributeTok{{-}f}\NormalTok{ scripts/02\_dev\_setup.sql}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This creates:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Production data tables (CLICKSTREAM\_DATA schema)
\item
  Incremental generator stored procedure and task
\item
  Development branch creation procedure
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{step-4-load-initial-data}{%
\subsection{Step 4: Load Initial Data}\label{step-4-load-initial-data}}

Use the Streamlit Data Manager or CLI to load sample data:

\section{Streamlit UI}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{cd}\NormalTok{ appendices/A\_sample\_data/data\_manager/}
\BuiltInTok{export} \VariableTok{SNOWFLAKE\_CONNECTION\_NAME}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{fs\_demo\_conn}
\ExtensionTok{streamlit}\NormalTok{ run app.py}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Update your connection config in
\texttt{\textasciitilde{}/.snowflake/connections.toml}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{[}\DataTypeTok{fs\_demo\_conn}\KeywordTok{]}
\DataTypeTok{account} \OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"your\_account"}
\DataTypeTok{user} \OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"FS\_DEMO\_USER"}
\DataTypeTok{role} \OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE"}
\DataTypeTok{warehouse} \OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"FS\_DEV\_WH"}
\DataTypeTok{database} \OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO"}
\DataTypeTok{schema} \OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"CLICKSTREAM\_DATA"}
\DataTypeTok{authenticator} \OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"externalbrowser"}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section{CLI}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{cd}\NormalTok{ appendices/A\_sample\_data/generator/}
\ExtensionTok{python}\NormalTok{ main.py }\AttributeTok{{-}{-}scale}\NormalTok{ 0.01 }\AttributeTok{{-}{-}database}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO }\AttributeTok{{-}{-}output}\NormalTok{ snowflake}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{step-5-consumer-quickstart-optional}{%
\subsection{Step 5: Consumer Quickstart
(Optional)}\label{step-5-consumer-quickstart-optional}}

Test consumer role capabilities:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{snowsql} \AttributeTok{{-}a} \OperatorTok{\textless{}}\NormalTok{account}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ {-}u FS\_DEMO\_USER }\AttributeTok{{-}r}\NormalTok{ FS\_CONSUMER\_ROLE }\AttributeTok{{-}f}\NormalTok{ scripts/03\_consumer\_quickstart.sql}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{security-model}{%
\section{Security Model}\label{security-model}}

\hypertarget{role-hierarchy}{%
\subsection{Role Hierarchy}\label{role-hierarchy}}

\begin{verbatim}
ACCOUNTADMIN
    └── SYSADMIN
            └── FS_ADMIN_ROLE
                    └── FS_DEV_ROLE
                            └── FS_CONSUMER_ROLE
\end{verbatim}

Each role inherits all privileges from roles below it. This means:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{DBA} can do everything DEV and Consumer can do
\item
  \textbf{DEV} can do everything Consumer can do
\item
  \textbf{Consumer} has the most restricted access
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{role-privileges-matrix}{%
\subsection{Role Privileges Matrix}\label{role-privileges-matrix}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lccc@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Capability & Consumer & Dev & DBA \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Query production data & ✅ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Query development data & ✅ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Create View-based Feature Views & ✅ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Generate training datasets & ✅ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Create personal schemas (dev) & ✅ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Create Dynamic Tables & ❌ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Create Tasks & ❌ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Suspend/Resume DTs & ❌ & ✅ & ✅ \\
Deploy incremental generator & ❌ & ❌ & ✅ \\
Create databases & ❌ & ❌ & ✅ \\
Promote to production & ❌ & ❌ & ✅ \\
Manage roles/privileges & ❌ & ❌ & ✅ \\
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{cost-control}{%
\subsection{Cost Control}\label{cost-control}}

The privilege model implements cost control by restricting who can
create cost-incurring objects:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Dynamic Tables}: Only DEV and DBA roles can create DTs. DTs
  incur ongoing compute costs for refresh.
\item
  \textbf{Tasks}: Only DEV and DBA roles can create scheduled tasks.
\item
  \textbf{Warehouses}: All roles can use warehouses, but only DBA can
  create or resize them.
\end{itemize}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-warning-color}{\faExclamationTriangle}\hspace{0.5em}{Consumer Role Restrictions}, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-warning-color!10!white, rightrule=.15mm, arc=.35mm, colframe=quarto-callout-warning-color-frame, left=2mm, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, breakable, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, opacitybacktitle=0.6, colback=white, coltitle=black, leftrule=.75mm]

The Consumer role is intentionally restricted from creating Dynamic
Tables and Tasks. This is a \textbf{cost control measure} - these
objects incur ongoing compute charges even when no one is actively using
them.

If a consumer needs DT-based features, they should request that a
developer create them.

\end{tcolorbox}

\hypertarget{database-organization}{%
\section{Database Organization}\label{database-organization}}

\hypertarget{feature_store_demo-production}{%
\subsection{FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO
(Production)}\label{feature_store_demo-production}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2963}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3704}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Schema
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Purpose
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Contents
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{CLICKSTREAM\_DATA} & Source data & Visitors, Users, Sessions,
Events, Orders \\
\texttt{CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN} & Generator admin & Config, State, Logs,
Task \\
\texttt{FEATURE\_STORE} & Production features & Entities, Feature
Views \\
\texttt{ML\_DATASETS} & Public datasets & Iris, Titanic, etc. \\
\texttt{TRAINING\_DATA} & Training outputs & Generated datasets \\
\texttt{INFERENCE\_DATA} & Inference data & Batch predictions \\
\texttt{SPINES} & Spine tables & Entity-timestamp combinations \\
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{feature_store_demo_dev-development}{%
\subsection{FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV
(Development)}\label{feature_store_demo_dev-development}}

The development database mirrors the production schema structure. Key
differences:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{CLICKSTREAM\_DATA}: Contains Dynamic Tables that sample from
  production
\item
  \textbf{FEATURE\_STORE}: Development feature views (can be modified
  freely)
\item
  \textbf{Personal schemas}: Consumers can create their own workspaces
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{multi-user-classroom-setup}{%
\section{Multi-User / Classroom
Setup}\label{multi-user-classroom-setup}}

For instructor-led training or workshops with multiple students:

\hypertarget{per-student-database-pattern}{%
\subsection{Per-Student Database
Pattern}\label{per-student-database-pattern}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Run as ACCOUNTADMIN}
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Create per{-}student databases}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{DATABASE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01;}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{DATABASE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT02;}
\CommentTok{{-}{-} ...}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Create per{-}student roles}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01\_ROLE;}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT02\_ROLE;}
\CommentTok{{-}{-} ...}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Grant ownership}
\KeywordTok{GRANT}\NormalTok{ OWNERSHIP }\KeywordTok{ON} \KeywordTok{DATABASE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01 }
    \KeywordTok{TO} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01\_ROLE;}
\CommentTok{{-}{-} ...}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Grant production read access to all student roles}
\KeywordTok{GRANT} \KeywordTok{USAGE} \KeywordTok{ON} \KeywordTok{DATABASE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO }\KeywordTok{TO} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01\_ROLE;}
\KeywordTok{GRANT} \KeywordTok{SELECT} \KeywordTok{ON} \KeywordTok{ALL} \KeywordTok{TABLES} \KeywordTok{IN} \KeywordTok{DATABASE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO }
    \KeywordTok{TO} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01\_ROLE;}
\CommentTok{{-}{-} ...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{benefits}{%
\subsection{Benefits}\label{benefits}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  All students query the same production data
\item
  Each student has isolated development environment
\item
  Students can't interfere with each other's work
\item
  Instructor can monitor all environments
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{creating-development-branches}{%
\section{Creating Development
Branches}\label{creating-development-branches}}

The \texttt{CREATE\_DEV\_BRANCH} procedure creates a development
database with Dynamic Tables that sample from production. You can
specify a custom database name for flexibility.

\hypertarget{basic-usage-1}{%
\subsection{Basic Usage}\label{basic-usage-1}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Create default dev branch (FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV)}
\KeywordTok{CALL}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN.CREATE\_DEV\_BRANCH();}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Create with custom settings}
\KeywordTok{CALL}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN.CREATE\_DEV\_BRANCH(}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,                    }\CommentTok{{-}{-} dev\_database}
    \FloatTok{10.0}\NormalTok{,                                }\CommentTok{{-}{-} sample\_pct (10\%)}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}1 HOUR\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,                           }\CommentTok{{-}{-} target\_lag}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FS\_DEV\_WH\textquotesingle{}}     \CommentTok{{-}{-} warehouse for DT refresh}
\NormalTok{);}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Create named branch (e.g., for a student or feature)}
\KeywordTok{CALL}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN.CREATE\_DEV\_BRANCH(}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
    \FloatTok{10.0}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}30 MINUTES\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FS\_DEV\_WH\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{);}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{managing-development-branches}{%
\subsection{Managing Development
Branches}\label{managing-development-branches}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Update refresh frequency for all DTs in a branch}
\KeywordTok{CALL}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN.UPDATE\_DEV\_BRANCH\_TARGET\_LAG(}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}15 MINUTES\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{);}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Drop a development branch}
\KeywordTok{CALL}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN.DROP\_DEV\_BRANCH(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV\_STUDENT01\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{);}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{what-gets-created}{%
\subsection{What Gets Created}\label{what-gets-created}}

The procedure creates:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Object & Type & Description \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{VISITORS} & Dynamic Table & Sampled visitors (root entity) \\
\texttt{USERS} & Dynamic Table & Users linked to sampled visitors \\
\texttt{SESSIONS} & Dynamic Table & Sessions for sampled visitors \\
\texttt{EVENTS} & Dynamic Table & Events for sampled sessions \\
\texttt{ORDERS} & Dynamic Table & Orders for sampled users \\
\texttt{ORDER\_ITEMS} & Dynamic Table & Items for sampled orders \\
\texttt{CATEGORIES} & Table & Full copy (rarely changes) \\
\texttt{SUPPLIERS} & Table & Full copy (rarely changes) \\
\texttt{PRODUCTS} & Table & Full copy (rarely changes) \\
\texttt{HOUSEHOLDS} & Table & Full copy (rarely changes) \\
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{git-integration}{%
\section{Git Integration}\label{git-integration}}

The setup includes a GitHub API integration that enables Git-backed
development workflows. This allows you to:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Create Git repositories linked to Snowflake schemas
\item
  Version control SQL scripts and stored procedures
\item
  Implement CI/CD pipelines for feature store promotion
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{creating-a-git-repository}{%
\subsection{Creating a Git Repository}\label{creating-a-git-repository}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Create a Git repository for feature store development}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{OR} \KeywordTok{REPLACE}\NormalTok{ GIT REPOSITORY FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV.FEATURE\_STORE.FEATURE\_REPO}
\NormalTok{    API\_INTEGRATION }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FS\_GIT\_API\_INTEGRATION}
\NormalTok{    ORIGIN }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}https://github.com/your{-}org/feature{-}store{-}repo.git\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{;}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} List branches}
\NormalTok{SHOW GIT BRANCHES }\KeywordTok{IN}\NormalTok{ GIT REPOSITORY FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV.FEATURE\_STORE.FEATURE\_REPO;}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Fetch latest changes}
\KeywordTok{ALTER}\NormalTok{ GIT REPOSITORY FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV.FEATURE\_STORE.FEATURE\_REPO FETCH;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{using-git-for-feature-definitions}{%

Definitions}\label{using-git-for-feature-definitions}}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} List files in the repository}
\NormalTok{LS @FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV.FEATURE\_STORE.FEATURE\_REPO}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{branches}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{main}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{GitHub App Authentication}, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, rightrule=.15mm, arc=.35mm, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, left=2mm, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, breakable, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, opacitybacktitle=0.6, colback=white, coltitle=black, leftrule=.75mm]

The API integration uses Snowflake's GitHub App for authentication.
Users will need to authorize the Snowflake GitHub App when first
connecting to a repository.

\end{tcolorbox}

\hypertarget{cicd-integration}{%

\begin{Shaded}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

See \protect\hyperlink{advanced-patterns}{Chapter 12: Advanced Patterns}
for detailed CI/CD patterns.

\hypertarget{troubleshooting-1}{%
\section{Troubleshooting}\label{troubleshooting-1}}

\hypertarget{common-issues}{%
\subsection{Common Issues}\label{common-issues}}

\textbf{``Insufficient privileges'' error}

Check your current role:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{SELECT}\NormalTok{ CURRENT\_ROLE();}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Switch to appropriate role:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{USE} \KeywordTok{ROLE}\NormalTok{ FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{``Object does not exist'' for warehouses}

Ensure DBA setup was run first:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{SHOW WAREHOUSES }\KeywordTok{LIKE} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FS\_\%\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Tasks not running}

Check task status:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{SHOW TASKS }\KeywordTok{IN} \KeywordTok{SCHEMA}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN;}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Resume if suspended}
\KeywordTok{ALTER}\NormalTok{ TASK FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_ADMIN.INCREMENTAL\_DATA\_TASK }\KeywordTok{RESUME}\NormalTok{;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Dynamic Tables not refreshing}

Check DT status:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{SELECT} \OperatorTok{*} \KeywordTok{FROM} \KeywordTok{TABLE}\NormalTok{(INFORMATION\_SCHEMA.DYNAMIC\_TABLE\_REFRESH\_HISTORY(}
\NormalTok{    NAME\_PREFIX }\OperatorTok{=\textgreater{}} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV.CLICKSTREAM\_DATA.\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{));}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{cleanup}{%
\subsection{Cleanup}\label{cleanup}}

To remove all objects created by this guide:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Run as ACCOUNTADMIN}
\KeywordTok{DROP} \KeywordTok{DATABASE} \ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO;}
\KeywordTok{DROP} \KeywordTok{DATABASE} \ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO\_DEV;}
\KeywordTok{DROP}\NormalTok{ INTEGRATION }\ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FS\_GIT\_API\_INTEGRATION;}
\KeywordTok{DROP}\NormalTok{ WAREHOUSE }\ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FS\_DEV\_WH;}
\KeywordTok{DROP} \FunctionTok{USER} \ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FS\_DEMO\_USER;}
\KeywordTok{DROP} \KeywordTok{ROLE} \ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FS\_CONSUMER\_ROLE;}
\KeywordTok{DROP} \KeywordTok{ROLE} \ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FS\_DEV\_ROLE;}
\KeywordTok{DROP} \KeywordTok{ROLE} \ControlFlowTok{IF} \KeywordTok{EXISTS}\NormalTok{ FS\_ADMIN\_ROLE;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{irreversible-action}{%
\section{Irreversible Action}\label{irreversible-action}}

The cleanup script permanently deletes all databases and their contents.
Make sure you have exported any data you need before running.

\part{Appendix C: Snowpark to Dynamic Table}

\hypertarget{snowpark-to-dynamic-table}{%
\chapter{Snowpark to Dynamic Table}\label{snowpark-to-dynamic-table}}

How Python features become SQL Dynamic Tables

\hfill\break

\hypertarget{overview-18}{%
\section{Overview}\label{overview-18}}

A common question when evaluating Snowflake Feature Store is:
\emph{``How does Python feature logic actually run inside a Dynamic
Table?''} This appendix explains the end-to-end path from Snowpark
DataFrame to Dynamic Table, the limitations that arise from that
translation, and optimisation strategies for production pipelines.

Examples that need a database and schema use the same \textbf{canonical
names} as the rest of this guide: database
\texttt{FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO}, Feature Store schema
\texttt{FEATURE\_STORE}, source schema \texttt{CLICKSTREAM\_DATA},
warehouse \texttt{FS\_DEV\_WH}, and FeatureView versions in
\textbf{\texttt{V01}} format (dynamic table suffix \texttt{\$V01}).

\hypertarget{the-translation-path}{%
\section{1. The Translation Path}\label{the-translation-path}}

When you define feature transformations in Python using Snowpark
DataFrames, \textbf{no Python code ships to the Dynamic Table}. The
pipeline works as follows:

\begin{verbatim}
┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│               SNOWPARK DATAFRAME → DYNAMIC TABLE PATH                        │
├──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                                                              │
│  DEVELOPMENT TIME                                                            │
│  ┌──────────────────┐    ┌──────────────────┐    ┌────────────────────────┐  │
│  │  Snowpark Python │───▶│  SQL Query Plan  │───▶│  FeatureView Object    │  │
│  │  DataFrame ops   │    │  (lazy, logical) │    │  (name, entity, freq)  │  │
│  └──────────────────┘    └──────────────────┘    └───────────┬────────────┘  │
│                                                              │               │
│  REGISTRATION TIME                                           ▼               │
│                                                  ┌────────────────────────┐  │
│                                                  │  CREATE DYNAMIC TABLE  │  │
│                                                  │  ... AS <generated SQL>│  │
│                                                  └───────────┬────────────┘  │
│                                                              │               │
│  RUNTIME (refresh cycles)                                    ▼               │
│                                                  ┌────────────────────────┐  │
│                                                  │  Pure SQL execution    │  │
│                                                  │  (no Python involved)  │  │
│                                                  └────────────────────────┘  │
│                                                                              │
└──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{step-1-snowpark-dataframe-lazy-evaluation}{%
\subsection{Step 1 -- Snowpark DataFrame (Lazy
Evaluation)}\label{step-1-snowpark-dataframe-lazy-evaluation}}

Snowpark DataFrames are \emph{lazily evaluated}. Each operation
(\texttt{filter}, \texttt{select}, \texttt{join}, \texttt{group\_by},
\texttt{agg}, etc.) appends to a logical query plan but does not execute
immediately. The Python syntax is a thin wrapper around SQL semantics.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ snowflake.ml.feature\_store }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ FeatureView, Entity}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ snowflake.snowpark.functions }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ F}

\NormalTok{user\_purchase\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    session.table(}\StringTok{"FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_DATA.ORDERS"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .group\_by(}\StringTok{"USER\_ID"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .agg(}
\NormalTok{        F.count\_distinct(}\StringTok{"ORDER\_ID"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"ORDER\_CNT"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        F.}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"TOTAL\_SPEND"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        F.avg(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"AVG\_ORDER\_AMT"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        F.}\BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"ORDER\_TS"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"LAST\_ORDER\_TS"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    )}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{step-2-query-pushdown-python-to-sql-translation}{%
\subsection{Step 2 -- Query Pushdown (Python-to-SQL
Translation)}\label{step-2-query-pushdown-python-to-sql-translation}}

When the DataFrame is consumed -- either by an action like
\texttt{collect()} or by passing it to a \texttt{FeatureView} --
Snowpark translates the entire chain of operations into a single
optimised SQL statement. You can inspect the generated SQL at any time:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(user\_purchase\_df.queries)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This produces the equivalent:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{SELECT}\NormalTok{ USER\_ID,}
       \FunctionTok{COUNT}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{DISTINCT}\NormalTok{ ORDER\_ID) }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ ORDER\_CNT,}
       \FunctionTok{SUM}\NormalTok{(AMOUNT)              }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ TOTAL\_SPEND,}
       \FunctionTok{AVG}\NormalTok{(AMOUNT)              }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ AVG\_ORDER\_AMT,}
       \FunctionTok{MAX}\NormalTok{(ORDER\_TS)            }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ LAST\_ORDER\_TS}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_DATA.ORDERS}
\KeywordTok{GROUP} \KeywordTok{BY}\NormalTok{ USER\_ID}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The translation is direct and deterministic: \texttt{F.sum()} becomes
\texttt{SUM()}, \texttt{F.col("X")\ /\ F.col("Y")} becomes
\texttt{X\ /\ Y}, \texttt{.filter()} becomes \texttt{WHERE},
\texttt{.join()} becomes \texttt{JOIN}, and so on. You can also write
SQL directly via \texttt{session.sql("SELECT\ ...")} -- the end result
is identical.

\hypertarget{step-3-featureview-registration-creates-a-dynamic-table}{%
\subsection{Step 3 -- FeatureView Registration Creates a Dynamic
Table}\label{step-3-featureview-registration-creates-a-dynamic-table}}

When you register a FeatureView with a \texttt{refresh\_freq}, the
Feature Store API issues a
\texttt{CREATE\ DYNAMIC\ TABLE\ ...\ AS\ \textless{}generated-SQL\textgreater{}}.
From that point forward, the Dynamic Table is a \textbf{pure SQL object}
-- no Python interpreter is involved in the refresh cycle.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{user\_purchase\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"USER\_PURCHASE\_STATS"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[user\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{user\_purchase\_df,}
\NormalTok{    timestamp\_col}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"LAST\_ORDER\_TS"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"15 minutes"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    desc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"User purchase statistics"}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{fs.register\_feature\_view(feature\_view}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{user\_purchase\_fv, version}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"V01"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Users who prefer to express feature-engineering logic directly in SQL
can do so by wrapping a raw SQL SELECT statement in
\texttt{session.sql()} and passing the resulting DataFrame as the
\texttt{feature\_df}. The outcome is identical -- the SQL text is used
as-is for the Dynamic Table definition:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{user\_purchase\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ session.sql(}\StringTok{"""}
\StringTok{    SELECT USER\_ID,}
\StringTok{           COUNT(DISTINCT ORDER\_ID) AS ORDER\_CNT,}
\StringTok{           SUM(AMOUNT)              AS TOTAL\_SPEND,}
\StringTok{           AVG(AMOUNT)              AS AVG\_ORDER\_AMT,}
\StringTok{           MAX(ORDER\_TS)            AS LAST\_ORDER\_TS}
\StringTok{    FROM FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_DATA.ORDERS}
\StringTok{    GROUP BY USER\_ID}
\StringTok{"""}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{user\_purchase\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"USER\_PURCHASE\_STATS"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[user\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{user\_purchase\_df,}
\NormalTok{    timestamp\_col}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"LAST\_ORDER\_TS"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"15 minutes"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    desc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"User purchase statistics"}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This is often preferable when teams already have well-tested SQL
transformations, or when the SQL is complex enough that the Snowpark
DataFrame API machine generated SQL adds verbosity without added
clarity.

\begin{quote}
\textbf{Key takeaway:} Python is a development-time convenience. At
runtime, the Dynamic Table refreshes by re-executing the generated SQL
with no Python involvement. Whether you author with Snowpark DataFrames
or raw SQL via \texttt{session.sql()}, the end result is the same.
\end{quote}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{limitations-what-can-and-cannot-be-expressed}{%
\section{2. Limitations: What Can and Cannot Be
Expressed}\label{limitations-what-can-and-cannot-be-expressed}}

Because the Dynamic Table is ultimately a SQL object, the constraints
come from what Dynamic Tables support in their query definition.

\hypertarget{incremental-vs-full-refresh}{%
\subsection{2.1 Incremental vs Full
Refresh}\label{incremental-vs-full-refresh}}

Dynamic Tables support three refresh modes:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Incremental} -- analyses the query to determine what changed
  since the last refresh and merges only those changes into the table.
\item
  \textbf{Full} -- re-executes the entire query from scratch and
  replaces the previously materialised results.
\item
  \textbf{AUTO} -- Snowflake evaluates the query at creation time and
  selects whichever mode it expects to be more cost- and time-effective.
\end{itemize}

Full refresh does not always imply slower or more expensive. The
relative cost depends on several factors:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Incrementalisation overhead.} Incremental refresh must track
  changes, compute deltas, and merge them -- this bookkeeping has its
  own cost. For simple queries over small-to-moderate tables, a
  straightforward full recomputation can be faster than the incremental
  machinery.
\item
  \textbf{Volume of changed data.} When a large proportion of rows
  change between refreshes (e.g., more than 5\% of grouping keys),
  incremental refresh can end up doing \emph{more} work than a full
  refresh because it recomputes each affected group individually.
\item
  \textbf{Data locality.} Operators like \texttt{GROUP\ BY},
  \texttt{DISTINCT}, \texttt{JOIN}, and window functions are sensitive
  to how well the source data is clustered by the relevant keys. Poor
  locality degrades incremental performance disproportionately.
\item
  \textbf{Query complexity.} Highly complex queries with many joins,
  subqueries, or nested CTEs may produce incremental plans that are
  harder for the optimizer to execute efficiently.
\end{itemize}

When using \textbf{AUTO} mode, Snowflake takes these trade-offs into
account at table creation time. If you are explicitly choosing a mode,
consider benchmarking both to see which performs better for your
specific workload.

Several SQL constructs \textbf{fall back to full refresh} or are
unsupported entirely:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2973}}
  >{\centering\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3514}}
  >{\centering\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1622}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1892}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Construct
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\centering
Incremental
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\centering
Full
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Notes
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{SELECT}, \texttt{WHERE}, \texttt{FROM} & Supported & Supported &
Performs consistently well \\
\texttt{GROUP\ BY} & Supported & Supported & Performance depends on data
locality \\
\texttt{INNER\ JOIN}, \texttt{OUTER\ JOIN} & Supported & Supported &
Best when one side is small or changes infrequently \\
\texttt{UNION\ ALL} & Supported & Supported & \\
\texttt{DISTINCT} & Supported & Supported & Locality-sensitive; prefer
\texttt{QUALIFY} (see below) \\

& Supported & Must include \texttt{PARTITION\ BY}; cluster source by
partition keys \\
\texttt{LATERAL\ FLATTEN} & Supported & Supported & \\
Cortex AI/LLM functions & Supported (SELECT clause) & Supported & \\

supported; must explicitly name output columns (no
\texttt{SELECT\ *}) \\

\texttt{SAMPLE} / \texttt{TABLESAMPLE} & \textbf{Not supported} &
\textbf{Not supported} & \\

& \\

Not supported & Supported & \texttt{UNION} supported incrementally \\

\texttt{WITH\ RECURSIVE} & Not supported & Supported & \\
\texttt{VOLATILE} UDFs & Not supported & Supported & \\
\end{longtable}

\begin{quote}
\textbf{Reference:}
\href{https://docs.snowflake.com/en/user-guide/dynamic-tables-supported-queries}{Supported
queries for dynamic tables}
\end{quote}

\hypertarget{python-udfs-and-udtfs-in-dynamic-tables}{%
\subsection{2.2 Python UDFs and UDTFs in Dynamic
Tables}\label{python-udfs-and-udtfs-in-dynamic-tables}}

UDFs and UDTFs provide a way to embed custom logic -- including Python
with third-party libraries -- inside a Dynamic Table's SQL definition.
They are \textbf{row-level transformations}: they operate on input
columns from a single row and produce output for that row. They do not
aggregate across rows or join between tables.

\textbf{UDF (scalar function):} Takes one or more input columns from a
single row and returns a \textbf{single output column}. The output can
be a scalar type (\texttt{FLOAT}, \texttt{STRING}, \texttt{INT}, etc.)
or a compound type (\texttt{VARIANT}, \texttt{OBJECT}, \texttt{ARRAY})
when multiple values need to be packed into one column. Typical
feature-engineering uses include:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Applying a pre-trained scoring model (n feature columns in, one score
  out)
\item
  Encoding or decoding a field using proprietary logic
\item
  Computing a derived value from multiple columns that cannot be
  expressed with native SQL functions
\item
  Parsing semi-structured data into a typed value
\end{itemize}

\textbf{UDTF (table function):} Takes one or more input columns from a
single row and returns \textbf{multiple output columns} (or multiple
output rows) per input row. Useful for exploding a \texttt{VARIANT} into
structured columns or reshaping row data.

\textbf{Vectorised execution.} UDFs and UDTFs can be declared as
vectorised, which means Snowflake sends batches of rows as Pandas
DataFrames to the Python runtime rather than invoking the function row
by row. This is an \textbf{execution-level optimisation} -- the logical
contract is still row-in, value(s)-out, but throughput improves
substantially because batch processing avoids per-row Python overhead.

If existing Python logic \emph{cannot} be expressed as Snowpark
DataFrame operations (e.g., proprietary libraries, complex numerical
algorithms), you can:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Register a Python UDF (or UDTF) in Snowflake.
\item
  Call it from within the Dynamic Table's SQL definition.
\end{enumerate}

According to the Snowflake documentation, both UDFs and UDTFs
\textbf{are supported for incremental refresh} (the docs list them as
separate entries in the support matrix). The DT refresh process applies
them only to changed rows, just as it would any other expression in the
SELECT clause. Neither inherently forces a full refresh.

\textbf{UDF caveats:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The UDF must be declared \textbf{\texttt{IMMUTABLE}} (not
  \texttt{VOLATILE}). \texttt{VOLATILE} UDFs are not supported for
  incremental refresh.
\item
  \textbf{Replacing} an \texttt{IMMUTABLE} UDF while it is in use by an
  incremental-refresh DT will cause refresh failures. The DT must be
  recreated after replacing the UDF.
\item
  UDFs that \textbf{import from an external stage} are not supported.
\item
  SQL UDFs that \textbf{contain subqueries} are not supported for
  incremental refresh.
\item
  \textbf{Vectorised UDFs} (processing Pandas DataFrames in batches)
  perform significantly better than scalar UDFs for large datasets.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{UDTF caveats:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{SQL UDTFs} are not supported in Dynamic Tables (only Python,
  Java, and Scala UDTFs).
\item
  SELECT blocks that read from a UDTF must \textbf{explicitly specify
  columns} -- \texttt{SELECT\ *} from a UDTF is not allowed.
\item
  As with UDFs, vectorised UDTFs are recommended for throughput.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ snowflake.snowpark.types }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ PandasSeries, FloatType}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ snowflake.snowpark.functions }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ udf, call\_udf}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}

\AttributeTok{@udf}\NormalTok{(name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"compute\_custom\_feature"}\NormalTok{, is\_permanent}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{     packages}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"pandas"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"numpy"}\NormalTok{], replace}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ compute\_custom\_feature(amount: PandasSeries[}\BuiltInTok{float}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{                           quantity: PandasSeries[}\BuiltInTok{int}\NormalTok{]) }\OperatorTok{{-}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ PandasSeries[}\BuiltInTok{float}\NormalTok{]:}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ amount }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.log1p(quantity)}

\NormalTok{feature\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ session.table(}\StringTok{"FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.CLICKSTREAM\_DATA.ORDERS"}\NormalTok{).select(}
\NormalTok{    F.col(}\StringTok{"USER\_ID"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    call\_udf(}\StringTok{"compute\_custom\_feature"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{             F.col(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{), F.col(}\StringTok{"QTY"}\NormalTok{)).alias(}\StringTok{"CUSTOM\_FEATURE"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{quote}
\textbf{Bottom line:} Existing Python logic can run via
\texttt{IMMUTABLE} UDFs within a Dynamic Table and still benefit from
incremental refresh. Prefer vectorised UDFs for performance, and avoid
replacing a UDF definition while a DT depends on it.
\end{quote}

\hypertarget{common-pattern-udf-for-incremental-batch-inference}{%
\subsection{2.2.1 Common Pattern: UDF for Incremental Batch
Inference}\label{common-pattern-udf-for-incremental-batch-inference}}

A particularly effective use of UDFs in Dynamic Tables is placing a
model-scoring UDF at the \textbf{end} of a FeatureView pipeline.
Upstream DTs prepare and aggregate the features incrementally; a final
DT calls a vectorised UDF that runs batch inference over those features.
Because the UDF is \texttt{IMMUTABLE} and the DT supports incremental
refresh, only the rows whose upstream features changed are scored --
giving you \textbf{incremental batch inference} without any external
orchestration.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Upstream FeatureView: features are incrementally maintained}
\NormalTok{user\_features\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"USER\_FEATURES"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[user\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{user\_features\_df,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"DOWNSTREAM"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Final FeatureView: scores new/changed rows via UDF}
\NormalTok{scored\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    session.table(}\StringTok{"FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.FEATURE\_STORE.USER\_FEATURES$V01"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .select(}
\NormalTok{        F.col(}\StringTok{"USER\_ID"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        F.col(}\StringTok{"TOTAL\_SPEND"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        F.col(}\StringTok{"ORDER\_CNT"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        call\_udf(}\StringTok{"predict\_churn\_score"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                 F.col(}\StringTok{"TOTAL\_SPEND"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{                 F.col(}\StringTok{"ORDER\_CNT"}\NormalTok{)).alias(}\StringTok{"CHURN\_SCORE"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    )}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{churn\_score\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"USER\_CHURN\_SCORES"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[user\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{scored\_df,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"1 hour"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    desc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Churn propensity scores {-} incremental batch inference"}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This pattern is well-suited to use cases like propensity scoring, risk
rating, or recommendation ranking where predictions need to stay fresh
as features update, but a full re-score of the entire population each
cycle would be wasteful.

\hypertarget{constructs-that-prevent-incremental-refresh-entirely}{%
\subsection{2.3 Constructs That Prevent Incremental Refresh
Entirely}\label{constructs-that-prevent-incremental-refresh-entirely}}

The following patterns force every refresh to be a full recomputation:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Non-deterministic functions in SELECT} (e.g.,
  \texttt{RANDOM()}, \texttt{CURRENT\_TIMESTAMP()} outside
  WHERE/HAVING/QUALIFY)
\item
  \textbf{Self-referential queries} (a DT reading its own previous
  state)
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{VOLATILE} UDFs} -- even if the logic is deterministic,
  the \texttt{VOLATILE} marker tells the optimizer it cannot be trusted
\item
  \textbf{Downstream incremental DTs cannot sit below upstream
  full-refresh DTs} -- an incremental DT is incompatible with the
  complete row changes that occur during each refresh of an upstream
  full-refresh table
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{view-based-featureviews-the-on-demand-alternative}{%
\section{3. View-Based FeatureViews: The On-Demand
Alternative}\label{view-based-featureviews-the-on-demand-alternative}}

Not every FeatureView needs a Dynamic Table. When you omit
\texttt{refresh\_freq}, the Feature Store creates a \textbf{view}
instead. The transformation SQL is stored as a view definition and
executed on-the-fly each time data is retrieved.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{product\_attributes\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"PRODUCT\_ATTRIBUTES"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[product\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{product\_df,}
\NormalTok{    timestamp\_col}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"UPDATED\_TS"}\NormalTok{,}
    \CommentTok{\# No refresh\_freq → View{-}based FeatureView}
\NormalTok{    desc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Product attributes {-} computed on query"}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{when-view-based-featureviews-make-sense}{%
\subsection{When View-Based FeatureViews Make
Sense}\label{when-view-based-featureviews-make-sense}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Prototyping and development.} During early iteration you want
  to change feature definitions rapidly without waiting for DT
  materialisation or paying continuous refresh costs. A view lets you
  test transformations immediately.
\item
  \textbf{Rarely accessed FeatureViews.} If a FeatureView is only
  consumed during periodic training runs (e.g., weekly or monthly), the
  cost of computing features on-the-fly at query time may be
  significantly lower than continuously maintaining a Dynamic Table.
\item
  \textbf{Slowly changing or static dimensions.} Reference data (product
  catalogues, region mappings) that changes infrequently often does not
  justify a DT.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{spine-filter-push-down}{%
\subsection{Spine Filter Push-Down}\label{spine-filter-push-down}}

A key advantage of view-based FeatureViews during dataset generation is
\textbf{spine filter push-down}. When the Feature Store performs an ASOF
join against a view-based FeatureView, the filters from the spine
(entity keys and timestamps) are pushed down into the view query. This
means the view \textbf{does not need to materialise its entire result
set} -- only the rows relevant to the spine are computed. This can
dramatically reduce compute cost for large source tables when the spine
is selective.

\hypertarget{escaping-dynamic-table-constraints-with-external-pipelines}{%
\subsection{Escaping Dynamic Table Constraints with External
Pipelines}\label{escaping-dynamic-table-constraints-with-external-pipelines}}

If a transformation pipeline is too complex or time-consuming to be
processed efficiently in Dynamic Tables -- for example, pipelines that
involve unsupported constructs, extensive Python library dependencies,
or orchestration logic that exceeds what a single SQL query can express
-- the transformations can be expressed in \textbf{any external tool}
(DBT, Airflow, Dagster, custom scripts, etc.). The resulting output
table is then registered as a \textbf{view-based FeatureView} that
simply references the externally maintained table:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# DBT (or any external tool) maintains the output table}
\CommentTok{\# Register it as a view{-}based FeatureView for FS integration}
\NormalTok{dbt\_features\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ session.table(}\StringTok{"FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.FEATURE\_STORE.USER\_STATS"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{dbt\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"USER\_STATS\_DBT"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[user\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{dbt\_features\_df,}
\NormalTok{    timestamp\_col}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"\_DBT\_UPDATED\_TS"}\NormalTok{,}
    \CommentTok{\# No refresh\_freq → View wrapping the externally managed table}
\NormalTok{    desc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"User statistics {-} maintained by DBT pipeline"}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{fs.register\_feature\_view(feature\_view}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{dbt\_fv, version}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"V01"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This gives you the full Feature Store benefits -- point-in-time-correct
dataset generation, entity-based retrieval, Model Registry lineage --
while keeping the transformation logic in the tool best suited for it.
The external tool owns the refresh schedule; the Feature Store owns the
feature contract.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{optimisation-strategies}{%
\section{4. Optimisation Strategies}\label{optimisation-strategies}}

\hypertarget{break-complex-pipelines-into-chained-dynamic-tables}{%
\subsection{4.1 Break Complex Pipelines into Chained Dynamic
Tables}\label{break-complex-pipelines-into-chained-dynamic-tables}}

Long, complex Snowpark pipelines translate to long, complex SQL, which
can degrade incremental refresh performance. The recommended pattern is
to \textbf{decompose} into multiple FeatureViews (each backed by its own
Dynamic Table) chained together:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Stage 1: Cleaning and parsing}
\NormalTok{clean\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ session.table(}\StringTok{"RAW\_EVENTS"}\NormalTok{).select(}
\NormalTok{    F.col(}\StringTok{"USER\_ID"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    F.to\_timestamp(}\StringTok{"EVENT\_TS"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"EVENT\_TS"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    F.col(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{).cast(}\StringTok{"FLOAT"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{clean\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"EVENTS\_CLEAN"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[user\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{clean\_df,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"DOWNSTREAM"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{fs.register\_feature\_view(feature\_view}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{clean\_fv, version}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"V01"}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Stage 2: Aggregation (reads from Stage 1\textquotesingle{}s DT)}
\NormalTok{agg\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    session.table(}\StringTok{"FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.FEATURE\_STORE.EVENTS\_CLEAN$V01"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .group\_by(}\StringTok{"USER\_ID"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .agg(F.}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"TOTAL\_SPEND"}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{agg\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"USER\_SPEND\_AGGREGATES"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[user\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{agg\_df,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"15 minutes"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{fs.register\_feature\_view(feature\_view}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{agg\_fv, version}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"V01"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Benefits:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Better incremental refresh} -- simpler SQL per DT means the
  optimizer can reason about changes more effectively.
\item
  \textbf{Independent monitoring} -- each stage has its own refresh
  history and health metrics.
\item
  \textbf{Mixed refresh strategies} -- fast refresh for one stage,
  slower for another.
\item
  \textbf{Full-refresh propagation awareness} -- any DT using full
  refresh forces all downstream DTs to also use full refresh (see
  Section 2.3). Design your chain so that stages using full-refresh
  constructs sit at the \emph{end} of the pipeline, not the beginning.
  If this is unavoidable, consider whether the full-refresh stages
  should instead be handled by an external pipeline and wrapped as a
  view-based FeatureView (see Section 3).
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{optimise-for-incremental-refresh}{%
\subsection{4.2 Optimise for Incremental
Refresh}\label{optimise-for-incremental-refresh}}

The Snowflake documentation provides specific guidance on operator-level
optimisation:

\textbf{Cluster source tables by grouping/join/partition keys.} Data
locality is the single biggest factor for incremental refresh
performance. When changes affect a small portion of grouping keys, the
DT only recomputes those groups.

\textbf{Keep changes to fewer than 5\% of grouping keys per refresh.}
When more than 5\% of keys change, incremental refresh may be slower
than full refresh.

\textbf{Use \texttt{QUALIFY\ ROW\_NUMBER()\ ...\ =\ 1} instead of
\texttt{DISTINCT} for deduplication.} The \texttt{QUALIFY} pattern has
an optimised incremental path that performs consistently faster than
\texttt{DISTINCT}, which is equivalent to \texttt{GROUP\ BY\ ALL}.

SQL:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Prefer this (optimised incremental path)}
\KeywordTok{SELECT}\NormalTok{ customer\_id, customer\_name, email, event\_time}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ customer\_events}
\NormalTok{QUALIFY }\FunctionTok{ROW\_NUMBER}\NormalTok{() }\KeywordTok{OVER}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{PARTITION} \KeywordTok{BY}\NormalTok{ customer\_id }\KeywordTok{ORDER} \KeywordTok{BY}\NormalTok{ event\_time }\KeywordTok{DESC}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Over this (locality{-}sensitive, potentially slow)}
\KeywordTok{SELECT} \KeywordTok{DISTINCT}\NormalTok{ customer\_id, customer\_name, email}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ customer\_events}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Snowpark equivalent:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ snowflake.snowpark.functions }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ row\_number}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ snowflake.snowpark }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ Window}

\CommentTok{\# Prefer this (optimised incremental path)}
\NormalTok{window }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Window.partition\_by(}\StringTok{"CUSTOMER\_ID"}\NormalTok{).order\_by(F.col(}\StringTok{"EVENT\_TIME"}\NormalTok{).desc())}

\NormalTok{deduped\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    session.table(}\StringTok{"CUSTOMER\_EVENTS"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .select(}\StringTok{"CUSTOMER\_ID"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"CUSTOMER\_NAME"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"EMAIL"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"EVENT\_TIME"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .with\_column(}\StringTok{"RN"}\NormalTok{, row\_number().over(window))}
\NormalTok{    .}\BuiltInTok{filter}\NormalTok{(F.col(}\StringTok{"RN"}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{==} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .drop(}\StringTok{"RN"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Over this (locality{-}sensitive, potentially slow)}
\NormalTok{distinct\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    session.table(}\StringTok{"CUSTOMER\_EVENTS"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .select(}\StringTok{"CUSTOMER\_ID"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"CUSTOMER\_NAME"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"EMAIL"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .distinct()}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Avoid compound expressions in \texttt{GROUP\ BY}.} Materialise
computed columns in an upstream DT first, then group on the simple
column reference in a downstream DT.

SQL:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Upstream DT: materialise the expression}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{DYNAMIC} \KeywordTok{TABLE}\NormalTok{ transactions\_with\_minute }\OperatorTok{..}\NormalTok{.}
\KeywordTok{AS} \KeywordTok{SELECT}\NormalTok{ DATE\_TRUNC(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}minute\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, ts) }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ ts\_minute, amount}
   \KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ transactions;}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Downstream DT: group on simple column}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{DYNAMIC} \KeywordTok{TABLE}\NormalTok{ minute\_sums }\OperatorTok{..}\NormalTok{.}
\KeywordTok{AS} \KeywordTok{SELECT}\NormalTok{ ts\_minute, }\FunctionTok{SUM}\NormalTok{(amount)}
   \KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ transactions\_with\_minute}
   \KeywordTok{GROUP} \KeywordTok{BY} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Snowpark equivalent (as two chained FeatureViews):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Upstream FeatureView: materialise the expression}
\NormalTok{stage1\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ session.table(}\StringTok{"TRANSACTIONS"}\NormalTok{).select(}
\NormalTok{    F.date\_trunc(}\StringTok{"minute"}\NormalTok{, F.col(}\StringTok{"TS"}\NormalTok{)).alias(}\StringTok{"TS\_MINUTE"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    F.col(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{stage1\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"TRANSACTIONS\_WITH\_MINUTE"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[txn\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{stage1\_df,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"DOWNSTREAM"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{fs.register\_feature\_view(feature\_view}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{stage1\_fv, version}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"V01"}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Downstream FeatureView: group on simple column}
\NormalTok{stage2\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    session.table(}\StringTok{"FEATURE\_STORE\_DEMO.FEATURE\_STORE.TRANSACTIONS\_WITH\_MINUTE$V01"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .group\_by(}\StringTok{"TS\_MINUTE"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    .agg(F.}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{).alias(}\StringTok{"TOTAL\_AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{stage2\_fv }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ FeatureView(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"MINUTE\_SUMS"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[txn\_entity],}
\NormalTok{    feature\_df}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{stage2\_df,}
\NormalTok{    refresh\_freq}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"15 minutes"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{fs.register\_feature\_view(feature\_view}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{stage2\_fv, version}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"V01"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{For joins, cluster the dimension table} (the side that changes
less often) by the join key. For \texttt{OUTER\ JOIN}s, put the table
that changes more frequently on the \texttt{LEFT} side. Clustering is
configured at the table level via SQL
(\texttt{ALTER\ TABLE\ ...\ CLUSTER\ BY\ ...}) and applies regardless of
whether you author the FeatureView in Snowpark or SQL.

\textbf{Always include \texttt{PARTITION\ BY} in window functions.}
Window functions without \texttt{PARTITION\ BY} result in full
recomputation of the entire result set on every refresh.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Good: partition\_by included}
\NormalTok{window }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Window.partition\_by(}\StringTok{"REGION"}\NormalTok{).order\_by(F.col(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{).desc())}
\NormalTok{ranked\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sales\_df.with\_column(}\StringTok{"SALES\_RANK"}\NormalTok{, F.rank().over(window))}

\CommentTok{\# Bad: no partition\_by {-}{-} forces full recomputation every refresh}
\NormalTok{window\_all }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Window.order\_by(F.col(}\StringTok{"AMOUNT"}\NormalTok{).desc())}
\NormalTok{ranked\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sales\_df.with\_column(}\StringTok{"GLOBAL\_RANK"}\NormalTok{, F.rank().over(window\_all))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{choose-an-appropriate-target-lag}{%
\subsection{4.3 Choose an Appropriate Target
Lag}\label{choose-an-appropriate-target-lag}}

Dynamic Tables do \textbf{not} blindly re-execute on every refresh
cycle. The scheduler checks for upstream changes and only triggers
warehouse compute when there is new data to process. This
change-detection check is near-zero cost, so a short target lag on a
source that updates infrequently does not waste significant compute --
the DT simply checks more often and finds nothing to do.

That said, target lag is still worth considering:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Shorter target lag} means the DT is ready to process changes
  sooner, giving lower latency from source change to feature
  availability. The compute cost is driven by the volume and frequency
  of actual data changes, not the target lag itself.
\item
  \textbf{Longer target lag} allows Snowflake to batch multiple small
  changes into fewer, larger refresh operations, which can be more
  efficient for high-frequency sources where micro-batching would
  otherwise create overhead.
\end{itemize}

Choose your target lag based on how quickly downstream consumers need to
see updated features, rather than trying to match it exactly to the
source update cadence.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2381}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2619}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Use Case
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Suggested Target Lag
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Rationale
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Real-time fraud / personalisation & \texttt{1\ minute} & Features must
reflect latest activity promptly \\
Operational ML features & \texttt{5-15\ minutes} & Balance of freshness
and batching efficiency \\
Standard reporting / training features & \texttt{1\ hour} & Consumers
tolerate moderate lag \\
Slowly changing dimensions & \texttt{1\ day} or greater & Source changes
are infrequent \\
\end{longtable}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{summary-13}{%
\section{5. Summary}\label{summary-13}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5556}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4444}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Question
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Answer
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
How does Python become a Dynamic Table? & Snowpark translates DataFrame
operations to SQL at registration time. The DT is a pure SQL object --
no Python runs during refresh. \\
Can we use existing Python code? & Yes, via \texttt{IMMUTABLE} UDFs
called within the DT definition. Immutable UDFs are supported for
incremental refresh. Prefer vectorised UDFs for performance, and avoid
replacing a UDF while a DT depends on it. \\
What are the limitations? & Certain SQL constructs don't support
incremental refresh (\texttt{PIVOT}, external functions,
\texttt{VOLATILE} UDFs, set operators other than
\texttt{UNION}/\texttt{UNION\ ALL}). See the
\href{https://docs.snowflake.com/en/user-guide/dynamic-tables-supported-queries}{supported
queries documentation} for the full matrix. \\
What about view-based FeatureViews? & Omit \texttt{refresh\_freq} to
create a view instead of a DT. The query runs on-the-fly at retrieval
time with spine filter push-down. Ideal for prototyping, rarely accessed
features, or wrapping externally managed tables (DBT, Airflow, etc.). \\
How do we optimise? & Break long pipelines into chained DTs, cluster
source data by grouping/join keys, use \texttt{QUALIFY} over
\texttt{DISTINCT}, and choose target lag based on how quickly consumers
need updated features. \\
\end{longtable}
